### Section: 15.2 Crystal Fractionation and Element Enrichment/Depletion in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In this section, we will discuss the process of crystal fractionation and how it leads to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. Crystal fractionation is the separation of minerals from a magma or solution due to differences in their chemical composition and density. This process plays a crucial role in the distribution of trace elements in volatile-rich systems.

#### 15.2a Definition and Mechanisms

Crystal fractionation occurs during the cooling and solidification of a magma or solution. As the temperature decreases, minerals with higher melting points will crystallize and separate from the remaining liquid. This process is driven by the differences in chemical composition and density between the minerals and the liquid. The minerals that crystallize first will be more enriched in certain trace elements, while the remaining liquid will become depleted in those elements.

There are several mechanisms that can lead to crystal fractionation in volatile-rich systems. These include fractional crystallization, assimilation, and magma mixing. Fractional crystallization is the most common mechanism and occurs when minerals with different chemical compositions crystallize at different temperatures. This process is driven by the principle of Bowen's reaction series, which states that minerals with higher melting points will crystallize first, followed by those with lower melting points. As the magma cools, the minerals that crystallize will become more enriched in certain trace elements, while the remaining liquid will become depleted in those elements.

Assimilation occurs when the magma or solution incorporates surrounding rocks, leading to changes in its chemical composition. This process can also lead to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in the magma or solution. Magma mixing, on the other hand, occurs when two or more magmas with different chemical compositions mix together. This can result in the formation of new minerals and the redistribution of trace elements.

#### 15.2b Role in Geochemical Cycles

Crystal fractionation plays a crucial role in the geochemical cycles of volatile-rich systems. As magma or solution cools and crystallizes, it can release volatile elements such as water, carbon dioxide, and sulfur dioxide. These volatile elements can then be incorporated into the atmosphere or hydrosphere, where they play important roles in various processes such as weathering, erosion, and climate regulation.

Furthermore, the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in the magma or solution can also have significant impacts on the surrounding environment. For example, the release of certain trace elements during volcanic eruptions can have detrimental effects on ecosystems and human health. On the other hand, the enrichment of certain trace elements in volcanic rocks can also be beneficial, as they can be used as indicators of mineral deposits.

In conclusion, crystal fractionation is a crucial process in volatile-rich systems that leads to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements. This process plays a significant role in the geochemical cycles of these systems and can have both positive and negative impacts on the surrounding environment. Understanding the mechanisms and effects of crystal fractionation is essential for deciphering the origin and evolution of volatile-rich systems.


## Chapter 15: Trace Element Abundance Variations in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In this chapter, we will explore the complex relationship between trace elements and volatile-rich systems. We will focus on the concept of volatile-rich equilibrium and how it relates to the fractional crystallization of trace elements in geological systems. This chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of the distribution and behavior of trace elements in volatile-rich systems, and how they can be used to decipher the origin and evolution of these systems.

### Section: 15.2 Crystal Fractionation and Element Enrichment/Depletion in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In this section, we will discuss the process of crystal fractionation and how it leads to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. Crystal fractionation is the separation of minerals from a magma or solution due to differences in their chemical composition and density. This process plays a crucial role in the distribution of trace elements in volatile-rich systems.

#### 15.2a Definition and Mechanisms

Crystal fractionation occurs during the cooling and solidification of a magma or solution. As the temperature decreases, minerals with higher melting points will crystallize and separate from the remaining liquid. This process is driven by the differences in chemical composition and density between the minerals and the liquid. The minerals that crystallize first will be more enriched in certain trace elements, while the remaining liquid will become depleted in those elements.

There are several mechanisms that can lead to crystal fractionation in volatile-rich systems. These include fractional crystallization, assimilation, and magma mixing. Fractional crystallization is the most common mechanism and occurs when minerals with different chemical compositions crystallize at different temperatures. This process is driven by the principle of Bowen's reaction series, which states that minerals with higher melting points will crystallize first, followed by those with lower melting points. This results in the separation of minerals with different chemical compositions and densities, leading to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in the remaining liquid.

Assimilation is another mechanism that can contribute to crystal fractionation in volatile-rich systems. It occurs when the magma or solution comes into contact with pre-existing rocks, causing the assimilation of their chemical components. This can lead to changes in the chemical composition of the magma or solution, resulting in the crystallization of different minerals and the enrichment or depletion of trace elements.

Magma mixing is a process that occurs when two or more magmas with different chemical compositions combine. This can result in the formation of a new magma with a different chemical composition, leading to the crystallization of different minerals and the enrichment or depletion of trace elements.

#### 15.2b Factors Affecting Crystal Fractionation

The process of crystal fractionation is influenced by several factors, including the composition of the magma or solution, the cooling rate, and the pressure and temperature conditions. The composition of the magma or solution plays a crucial role in determining which minerals will crystallize first and the extent of trace element enrichment or depletion. The cooling rate also affects crystal fractionation, as a slower cooling rate allows for more time for minerals to crystallize and separate from the remaining liquid. Pressure and temperature conditions can also impact crystal fractionation, as changes in these conditions can alter the melting points of minerals and affect their crystallization sequence.

### Subsection: 15.2c Case Studies

To further illustrate the process of crystal fractionation and its effects on trace element distribution in volatile-rich systems, we will examine two case studies: the formation of the Sudbury Igneous Complex and the eruption of Mount St. Helens in 1980.

The Sudbury Igneous Complex is a large impact crater in Ontario, Canada, formed by a meteorite impact approximately 1.85 billion years ago. The impact melted the surrounding rocks and formed a large magma chamber, which underwent extensive crystal fractionation. As a result, the Sudbury Igneous Complex is known for its unique mineral assemblages and high concentrations of nickel, copper, and platinum group elements.

The eruption of Mount St. Helens in 1980 also provides a prime example of crystal fractionation in action. Prior to the eruption, the magma chamber beneath the volcano underwent extensive crystal fractionation, resulting in the formation of different mineral layers. As the magma chamber continued to cool and crystallize, the remaining liquid became enriched in volatile elements such as sulfur and chlorine. This led to a highly explosive eruption, as the sudden release of these volatile elements caused the magma to fragment and erupt violently.

These case studies demonstrate the significant role that crystal fractionation plays in the distribution of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. By understanding this process, we can gain valuable insights into the origin and evolution of these systems and their impact on the Earth's geology and environment.


### Section: 15.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In the previous section, we discussed how crystal fractionation can lead to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. However, the process of crystal fractionation is not the only factor that affects the distribution of trace elements. Pressure and temperature also play a crucial role in trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems.

#### 15.3a Basic Principles

The distribution of trace elements in volatile-rich systems is strongly influenced by pressure and temperature. This is because pressure and temperature affect the physical and chemical properties of minerals and liquids, which in turn, influence their ability to incorporate or exclude trace elements.

At high pressures, minerals tend to have lower solubility for trace elements, meaning they are less likely to incorporate them into their crystal structure. This is due to the increased density and decreased volume of the mineral at high pressures, which makes it more difficult for trace elements to fit into the crystal lattice. On the other hand, at high temperatures, minerals tend to have higher solubility for trace elements, as the increased thermal energy allows for more space in the crystal lattice for trace elements to fit.

The effect of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning can also be seen in the behavior of volatile elements, such as water and carbon dioxide. At high pressures, these volatile elements are more soluble in the melt, leading to their enrichment in the remaining liquid. However, at high temperatures, these volatile elements become less soluble and are more likely to be incorporated into minerals during crystallization.

In addition to pressure and temperature, the composition of the melt also plays a significant role in trace element partitioning. Different minerals have different affinities for certain trace elements, and the composition of the melt can influence which minerals will crystallize first and therefore, which trace elements will be enriched or depleted in the remaining liquid.

Understanding the effect of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning is crucial for interpreting the geochemical signatures of volatile-rich systems. By considering these factors, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that control the distribution of trace elements and use this information to decipher the origin and evolution of these systems.


### Section: 15.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In the previous section, we discussed how crystal fractionation can lead to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. However, the process of crystal fractionation is not the only factor that affects the distribution of trace elements. Pressure and temperature also play a crucial role in trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems.

#### 15.3b Experimental Studies

To better understand the effects of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning, numerous experimental studies have been conducted. These studies involve subjecting synthetic melts or natural rock samples to varying pressures and temperatures and analyzing the resulting trace element concentrations in the minerals and liquids.

One such study by Blundy and Wood (1994) examined the partitioning of trace elements between olivine and basaltic melt at different pressures and temperatures. They found that at high pressures, the olivine crystals had lower concentrations of trace elements, while the melt had higher concentrations. This is consistent with the basic principles discussed in the previous section, where minerals have lower solubility for trace elements at high pressures.

Another study by Dasgupta et al. (2007) looked at the partitioning of volatile elements, such as water and carbon dioxide, between silicate melts and minerals at high pressures and temperatures. They found that at high pressures, these volatile elements were more soluble in the melt, leading to their enrichment in the remaining liquid. However, at high temperatures, these volatile elements became less soluble and were more likely to be incorporated into minerals during crystallization.

These experimental studies have provided valuable insights into the behavior of trace elements in volatile-rich systems and have helped to refine our understanding of the effects of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning.

#### 15.3c Composition of the Melt

In addition to pressure and temperature, the composition of the melt also plays a significant role in trace element partitioning. Different minerals have different affinities for certain trace elements, and the composition of the melt can influence which minerals will crystallize and which trace elements will be incorporated into them.

For example, in a basaltic melt, olivine has a higher affinity for magnesium and iron, while clinopyroxene has a higher affinity for calcium and aluminum. This means that at high pressures and temperatures, where olivine is more likely to crystallize, the remaining melt will be enriched in calcium and aluminum. This can have significant implications for the trace element composition of volcanic rocks, as the composition of the melt can vary depending on the source of the magma and the conditions under which it formed.

#### 15.3d Applications in Volcanic Studies

The understanding of the effects of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems has important applications in volcanic studies. By analyzing the trace element composition of volcanic rocks, researchers can gain insights into the conditions under which the magma formed and the processes that influenced its evolution.

For example, the presence of certain trace elements, such as water and carbon dioxide, can indicate the depth at which the magma formed and the extent of degassing that occurred during its ascent. Additionally, the distribution of trace elements between minerals and melt can provide information about the crystallization history of the magma and the processes that led to its enrichment or depletion in certain elements.

In conclusion, pressure and temperature are crucial factors in trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems. Experimental studies and the understanding of the composition of the melt have helped to refine our understanding of these processes and their applications in volcanic studies. 


### Section: 15.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Volatile-Rich Systems:

In the previous section, we discussed how crystal fractionation can lead to the enrichment or depletion of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. However, the process of crystal fractionation is not the only factor that affects the distribution of trace elements. Pressure and temperature also play a crucial role in trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems.

#### 15.3c Implications for Geochemical Processes

The experimental studies discussed in the previous section have provided valuable insights into the behavior of trace elements in volatile-rich systems. These insights have important implications for understanding various geochemical processes that occur in these systems.

One such process is magma differentiation, which involves the separation of a melt into different compositions due to the crystallization of minerals. As discussed in the previous section, pressure and temperature have a significant impact on the partitioning of trace elements between minerals and melts. This means that the composition of a magma can change significantly as it undergoes differentiation, leading to variations in trace element abundances.

Another important process affected by pressure and temperature is the formation of ore deposits. Many valuable minerals and elements are found in volatile-rich systems, and their distribution is often controlled by the partitioning of trace elements. For example, the enrichment of certain trace elements in the remaining melt during magma differentiation can lead to the formation of economically important ore deposits.

Furthermore, the behavior of volatile elements, such as water and carbon dioxide, at high pressures and temperatures has implications for the formation of hydrothermal systems. These systems are important for the transport and concentration of elements in the Earth's crust, and their formation is influenced by the solubility of volatile elements in melts and minerals.

In addition to these processes, the understanding of trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems has implications for the interpretation of geochemical data. By considering the effects of pressure and temperature on trace element distribution, we can better interpret the geochemical signatures of rocks and minerals and gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped them.

In conclusion, the study of trace element partitioning in volatile-rich systems is crucial for understanding various geochemical processes and interpreting geochemical data. The experimental studies discussed in this chapter have provided valuable insights into the behavior of trace elements under different pressure and temperature conditions, and further research in this area will continue to enhance our understanding of these complex systems.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the variations in trace element abundance in volatile-rich systems. We have seen how the presence of volatiles can greatly affect the distribution and behavior of trace elements in geological systems. We have also discussed the various processes that can lead to these variations, such as degassing, fluid-rock interactions, and magma differentiation. Through the use of case studies and examples, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complex relationship between volatiles and trace elements.

One key takeaway from this chapter is the importance of considering volatiles when studying trace element geochemistry. The presence of volatiles can significantly alter the composition of a system and can even lead to the formation of unique mineral assemblages. Therefore, it is crucial for geochemists to carefully consider the role of volatiles in their research and to account for their effects when interpreting data.

In conclusion, the study of trace element abundance variations in volatile-rich systems is a complex and fascinating field of geochemistry. By understanding the processes and factors that influence these variations, we can gain valuable insights into the evolution of geological systems and the behavior of trace elements. As we continue to advance our knowledge and techniques in this area, we can expect to uncover even more intriguing discoveries.

## Chapter: Trace-Element Geochemistry Textbook
### Introduction:

In this chapter, we will explore the variations in trace element abundance in magma-rich systems. Trace elements are those that occur in very small concentrations in rocks and minerals, typically less than 0.1% by weight. Despite their low abundance, trace elements play a crucial role in understanding the geochemical processes that occur in magmatic systems. These elements can provide valuable information about the source of the magma, the conditions under which it formed, and the processes that have affected it since its formation.

We will begin by discussing the sources of trace elements in magmatic systems, including the role of the mantle, crust, and other sources such as fluids and sediments. We will then delve into the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas, including fractional crystallization, assimilation, and magma mixing. We will also explore the effects of these processes on the trace element composition of magmas and how they can be used to interpret the evolution of magmatic systems.

Next, we will examine the various analytical techniques used to measure trace element abundances in magmas, including X-ray fluorescence (XRF), inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), and laser ablation inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (LA-ICP-MS). We will discuss the advantages and limitations of each technique and how they have contributed to our understanding of trace element geochemistry in magmatic systems.

Finally, we will apply our knowledge of trace element geochemistry to real-world examples, including the trace element variations observed in different types of magmatic systems such as mid-ocean ridges, island arcs, and continental rifts. We will also explore the role of trace elements in the formation of economic mineral deposits and how they can be used as exploration tools.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of the variations in trace element abundance in magma-rich systems and how they can be used to interpret the processes that have shaped our planet's geology. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of trace element geochemistry in magmatic systems.


### Related Context

### Section: 16.1 Magma-Rich Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Magma-rich systems are characterized by the presence of large amounts of magma, which can range from a few percent to over 90% of the total rock volume. These systems are typically found in areas of active volcanism, such as mid-ocean ridges, island arcs, and continental rifts. The high concentration of magma in these systems provides a unique opportunity to study the behavior of trace elements during magmatic processes.

#### 16.1a Basic Concepts

Before delving into the specifics of magma-rich equilibrium and fractional crystallization, it is important to understand some basic concepts related to trace element geochemistry. First, it is important to note that trace elements are not evenly distributed throughout the Earth's crust and mantle. Instead, they are concentrated in certain minerals and rocks, and their distribution is controlled by a variety of factors such as mineral stability, melting processes, and fluid interactions.

Second, the behavior of trace elements during magmatic processes is largely controlled by their chemical properties. For example, elements with similar ionic radii and charge tend to behave similarly during melting and crystallization processes. This is known as the "compatibility principle" and is a key concept in understanding the distribution of trace elements in magmatic systems.

Finally, it is important to note that the behavior of trace elements can be influenced by a variety of factors, including temperature, pressure, and the presence of other elements. This means that the distribution of trace elements in magmatic systems can vary significantly depending on the specific conditions of the system.

Now that we have a basic understanding of trace element geochemistry, let's explore the concept of magma-rich equilibrium and fractional crystallization.

### Introduction:

In this chapter, we will explore the variations in trace element abundance in magma-rich systems. ### Section: 16.1 Magma-Rich Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Magma-rich systems are characterized by the presence of large amounts of molten rock, or magma, which can vary in composition and temperature. As magma cools and solidifies, it undergoes a process known as fractional crystallization, where minerals with higher melting points crystallize and are removed from the melt. This process can lead to significant variations in the trace element composition of the remaining melt.

#### 16.1b Role in Trace Element Distribution

Fractional crystallization plays a crucial role in the distribution of trace elements in magmas. As minerals crystallize and are removed from the melt, they can incorporate trace elements into their crystal structure. This results in a decrease in the concentration of these elements in the remaining melt. The extent of this decrease depends on the compatibility of the trace element with the mineral phase, as well as the degree of fractional crystallization.

In addition to affecting the concentration of trace elements, fractional crystallization can also lead to changes in the relative abundances of different trace elements. This is because different minerals have varying affinities for different trace elements, leading to preferential incorporation of certain elements into specific mineral phases. As a result, the trace element composition of the remaining melt can be significantly different from the initial composition of the magma.

The role of fractional crystallization in trace element distribution can also be influenced by other processes, such as magma mixing and assimilation. Magma mixing occurs when two or more magmas with different compositions combine, leading to the formation of a hybrid magma. This can result in changes in the trace element composition of the resulting magma, as well as the formation of new mineral phases that can incorporate trace elements.

Assimilation, on the other hand, occurs when the magma interacts with and incorporates material from the surrounding rocks. This can also lead to changes in the trace element composition of the magma, as well as the formation of new mineral phases. The extent of assimilation can vary depending on the composition of the surrounding rocks and the temperature and viscosity of the magma.

In summary, fractional crystallization plays a crucial role in the distribution of trace elements in magma-rich systems. It can lead to significant changes in the concentration and relative abundances of trace elements in the remaining melt, providing valuable information about the evolution of magmatic systems. However, it is important to consider the potential influence of other processes, such as magma mixing and assimilation, on the trace element composition of magmas.

### Section: 16.1 Magma-Rich Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Magma-rich systems are characterized by the presence of large amounts of molten rock, or magma, which can vary in composition and temperature. The composition of magma is determined by the source of the melt, which can be the mantle, crust, or other sources. As magma cools and crystallizes, it undergoes chemical and physical changes that can result in the formation of different minerals and the partitioning of trace elements between the liquid and solid phases.

#### 16.1c Case Studies

To better understand the processes that control trace element abundance variations in magma-rich systems, we will examine several case studies. These case studies will highlight the role of fractional crystallization and other processes in shaping the trace element composition of magmas.

One such case study is the Hawaiian Islands, which are formed by the eruption of basaltic magma from a hot spot in the Earth's mantle. As the magma rises to the surface, it undergoes fractional crystallization, with olivine and pyroxene crystals forming first, followed by plagioclase and other minerals. This process results in a decrease in the abundance of incompatible trace elements, such as the rare earth elements (REEs), in the remaining liquid magma.

Another example is the formation of granitic magmas in continental crust. These magmas are formed by the partial melting of existing rocks, which can result in the enrichment of certain trace elements, such as uranium and thorium. As the magma cools and crystallizes, these elements can become concentrated in specific minerals, such as zircon and monazite.

These case studies demonstrate the importance of understanding the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas. By studying the trace element composition of magmas, we can gain insights into the source of the magma, the conditions under which it formed, and the processes that have affected it since its formation.

### Section: 16.2 Crystal Fractionation and Element Enrichment/Depletion in Magma-Rich Systems:

Crystal fractionation is a process that occurs during the cooling and crystallization of magma, where certain minerals crystallize and are removed from the melt, causing changes in the chemical composition of the remaining melt. This process can lead to both enrichment and depletion of trace elements in the magma.

#### 16.2a Definition and Mechanisms

Crystal fractionation is the process by which minerals crystallize from a magma as it cools, leading to changes in the chemical composition of the remaining melt. This process is driven by the differences in the chemical and physical properties of the minerals and the melt. As the magma cools, minerals with higher melting points will crystallize first, leaving behind a melt that is enriched in elements with lower melting points.

There are several mechanisms by which crystal fractionation can occur, including fractional crystallization, equilibrium crystallization, and batch crystallization. Fractional crystallization is the most common mechanism and involves the sequential crystallization of minerals from the melt. Equilibrium crystallization occurs when the minerals and melt are in chemical equilibrium, and the composition of the melt remains constant as minerals crystallize. Batch crystallization occurs when a batch of crystals is removed from the melt, causing changes in the chemical composition of the remaining melt.

Crystal fractionation can lead to both enrichment and depletion of trace elements in the magma. Enrichment occurs when minerals that are rich in certain trace elements crystallize and are removed from the melt, leaving behind a melt that is enriched in those elements. Depletion occurs when minerals that are poor in certain trace elements crystallize and are removed from the melt, leaving behind a melt that is depleted in those elements.

In addition to crystal fractionation, other processes such as assimilation and magma mixing can also affect the trace element composition of magmas. Assimilation occurs when the magma incorporates material from the surrounding rocks, which can introduce new trace elements into the melt. Magma mixing occurs when two or more magmas with different compositions mix together, leading to changes in the trace element composition of the resulting magma.

Understanding the mechanisms and effects of crystal fractionation and other processes on trace element abundance in magmas is crucial for interpreting the evolution of magmatic systems. By studying the trace element composition of magmas, we can gain valuable insights into the source of the magma, the conditions under which it formed, and the processes that have affected it since its formation.

### Section: 16.2 Crystal Fractionation and Element Enrichment/Depletion in Magma-Rich Systems:

Crystal fractionation is a process that occurs during the cooling and crystallization of magma, where certain minerals crystallize and are removed from the melt, causing changes in the chemical composition of the remaining melt. This process can lead to both enrichment and depletion of trace elements in the magma.

#### 16.2b Role in Geochemical Cycles

Crystal fractionation plays a significant role in the geochemical cycles of trace elements. As magma cools and crystallizes, certain minerals preferentially incorporate certain trace elements, leading to their enrichment or depletion in the remaining melt. This can have a cascading effect on the geochemical cycles of these elements, as they are transported and recycled through the Earth's crust and mantle.

For example, during the formation of oceanic crust, crystal fractionation can lead to the enrichment of certain trace elements in the remaining melt, which can then be transported to the surface through volcanic eruptions. These elements can then be incorporated into the oceanic crust and eventually subducted back into the mantle, where they may undergo further crystal fractionation processes.

Crystal fractionation can also play a role in the formation of ore deposits, as certain trace elements can be concentrated in specific minerals during the crystallization of magma. This can lead to the formation of economically valuable deposits of elements such as copper, gold, and platinum.

Overall, understanding the role of crystal fractionation in trace element geochemistry is crucial for understanding the larger geochemical cycles of these elements and their impact on the Earth's crust and mantle.

## Chapter: Trace-Element Geochemistry Textbook
### Introduction:

In this chapter, we will explore the variations in trace element abundance in magma-rich systems. We will begin by discussing the sources of trace elements in magmatic systems, including the role of the mantle, crust, and other sources such as fluids and sediments. We will then delve into the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas, including fractional crystallization, assimilation, and magma mixing. We will also explore the effects of these processes on the trace element composition of magmas and how they can be used to interpret the evolution of magmatic systems.

### Section: 16.2 Crystal Fractionation and Element Enrichment/Depletion in Magma-Rich Systems:

Crystal fractionation is a process that occurs during the cooling and crystallization of magma, where minerals with different chemical compositions crystallize at different rates. This results in the separation of elements into different mineral phases, leading to changes in the trace element composition of the remaining melt. This process can also lead to the enrichment or depletion of certain trace elements in the magma.

One example of crystal fractionation and element enrichment can be seen in the formation of granitic plutons. As magma cools and crystallizes, minerals such as biotite and amphibole preferentially incorporate certain trace elements, such as potassium and thorium, into their crystal structure. This results in the remaining melt becoming enriched in incompatible elements, leading to the formation of a granitic pluton with a distinct trace element signature.

On the other hand, crystal fractionation can also lead to element depletion in certain magmas. This is often seen in basaltic magmas, where olivine and pyroxene crystals preferentially incorporate elements such as magnesium and iron, resulting in the remaining melt becoming depleted in these elements. This process can also lead to the formation of more evolved magmas, such as andesites and rhyolites, which have a different trace element composition compared to the original basaltic magma.

### Subsection: 16.2c Case Studies

To further illustrate the role of crystal fractionation in trace element geochemistry, we will examine two case studies: the formation of the Skaergaard intrusion and the eruption of Mount St. Helens in 1980.

The Skaergaard intrusion, located in Greenland, is a layered mafic intrusion that formed approximately 55 million years ago. Through the process of crystal fractionation, the magma chamber became enriched in incompatible elements such as potassium, thorium, and uranium, resulting in the formation of a highly differentiated magma. This magma eventually solidified into a layered intrusion, with different mineral layers containing varying concentrations of trace elements.

In contrast, the eruption of Mount St. Helens in 1980 provides an example of crystal fractionation leading to element depletion. Prior to the eruption, the magma chamber beneath the volcano underwent extensive crystal fractionation, resulting in the formation of a highly evolved magma. This magma was depleted in certain elements, such as iron and magnesium, which led to the explosive eruption of the volcano.

These case studies highlight the importance of crystal fractionation in controlling the trace element composition of magmas and the resulting geological processes. By studying the trace element variations in these systems, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that shape our planet and the evolution of magmatic systems.

### Section: 16.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Magma-Rich Systems:

As magmas rise towards the Earth's surface, they experience changes in pressure and temperature that can significantly affect the distribution of trace elements. In this section, we will explore the basic principles of trace element partitioning and how pressure and temperature can influence this process.

#### 16.3a Basic Principles

Trace element partitioning refers to the distribution of trace elements between different phases in a magma. This process is controlled by the relative affinities of the trace elements for different phases, as well as the physical and chemical conditions of the magma. The partitioning coefficient, Kd, is a measure of the relative affinity of a trace element for a particular phase and is defined as the ratio of the concentration of the element in the solid phase to the concentration in the liquid phase.

The partitioning coefficient is influenced by a number of factors, including the composition of the magma, the temperature and pressure conditions, and the mineralogy of the solid phases. For example, in a magma with a high silica content, trace elements tend to be more compatible with the liquid phase, while in a more mafic magma, they may be more compatible with the solid phases.

Pressure and temperature can also have a significant impact on trace element partitioning. As pressure increases, the solubility of trace elements in the liquid phase decreases, leading to a higher partitioning coefficient. This means that more trace elements will be incorporated into the solid phases as pressure increases. Similarly, as temperature increases, the solubility of trace elements in the liquid phase also increases, leading to a lower partitioning coefficient.

The effect of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning can be seen in the distribution of elements between different mineral phases in a magma. For example, in a basaltic magma, olivine is the first mineral to crystallize at high temperatures and pressures. As the magma cools and pressure decreases, plagioclase feldspar and pyroxene begin to crystallize. These minerals have a higher affinity for certain trace elements, leading to their preferential incorporation into the solid phases.

In summary, pressure and temperature play a crucial role in controlling the distribution of trace elements in magmas. Understanding the basic principles of trace element partitioning and how they are affected by these factors is essential for interpreting the geochemical evolution of magmatic systems. In the next section, we will explore specific examples of how pressure and temperature can influence trace element partitioning in different types of magmas.

### Section: 16.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Magma-Rich Systems:

As magmas rise towards the Earth's surface, they experience changes in pressure and temperature that can significantly affect the distribution of trace elements. In this section, we will explore the experimental studies that have been conducted to understand how pressure and temperature influence trace element partitioning in magma-rich systems.

#### 16.3b Experimental Studies

Experimental studies involve subjecting synthetic magmas to varying pressure and temperature conditions in a controlled laboratory setting. These experiments allow us to observe how trace elements behave under different conditions and provide valuable insights into the processes that control their distribution in natural magmatic systems.

One of the key findings from experimental studies is that pressure and temperature have a significant impact on the partitioning of trace elements between different phases in a magma. For example, at higher pressures, trace elements tend to partition into the solid phase (crystals) rather than the liquid phase (melt). This is because the solubility of trace elements in the melt decreases with increasing pressure, causing them to preferentially incorporate into the growing crystals.

Additionally, temperature can also affect the partitioning of trace elements. At higher temperatures, the solubility of trace elements in the melt increases, leading to a higher concentration of these elements in the liquid phase. This can result in a more homogeneous distribution of trace elements in the magma, as they are less likely to partition into the solid phase.

Experimental studies have also shown that the composition of the magma can influence the partitioning of trace elements. For example, the presence of certain minerals or fluids can enhance or inhibit the incorporation of trace elements into the solid phase. This highlights the importance of considering the overall composition of a magma when interpreting trace element data.

In conclusion, experimental studies have provided valuable insights into the effect of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning in magma-rich systems. These findings have greatly improved our understanding of the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas and have allowed us to make more accurate interpretations of geochemical data.

We will begin by discussing the sources of trace elements in magmatic systems, including the role of the mantle, crust, and other sources such as fluids and sediments. We will then delve into the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas, including fractional crystallization, assimilation, and magma mixing. We will also explore the effects of these processes on the trace element composition of magmas and how they can be used to interpret the evolution of magmatic systems.

Next, we will examine the various analytical techniques used to measure trace element abundances in magmas, including X-ray fluorescence (XRF), inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), and laser ablation inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (LA-ICP-MS). We will discuss the advantages and limitations of each technique and how they have contributed to our understanding of trace element geochemistry.

### Section: 16.3 Effect of Pressure and Temperature on Trace Element Partitioning in Magma-Rich Systems:

As magmas rise towards the Earth's surface, they experience changes in pressure and temperature that can significantly affect the distribution of trace elements. In this section, we will explore how pressure and temperature influence the partitioning of trace elements between different phases in magma-rich systems.

#### 16.3a Pressure Effects on Trace Element Partitioning:

The pressure at which a magma forms can have a significant impact on the distribution of trace elements between the liquid and solid phases. As pressure increases, the solubility of trace elements in the liquid phase decreases, leading to their preferential partitioning into solid phases. This effect is known as pressure-dependent partitioning and can result in significant variations in trace element abundances in magmas formed at different depths.

One example of this is the enrichment of incompatible elements, such as the rare earth elements (REEs), in magmas that form at shallow depths. As the pressure decreases during magma ascent, these elements become increasingly incompatible and are preferentially partitioned into the liquid phase. This can result in significant enrichments of these elements in volcanic rocks compared to their source rocks.

#### 16.3b Temperature Effects on Trace Element Partitioning:

Temperature also plays a crucial role in the partitioning of trace elements in magmas. As temperature increases, the solubility of trace elements in the liquid phase also increases, leading to their preferential partitioning into the liquid phase. This effect is known as temperature-dependent partitioning and can result in significant variations in trace element abundances in magmas formed at different temperatures.

One example of this is the depletion of incompatible elements, such as the REEs, in magmas that form at high temperatures. As the temperature increases, these elements become increasingly compatible and are preferentially partitioned into the solid phase. This can result in significant depletions of these elements in volcanic rocks compared to their source rocks.

### Subsection: 16.3c Implications for Geochemical Processes

The pressure and temperature effects on trace element partitioning have important implications for understanding geochemical processes in magmatic systems. By examining the variations in trace element abundances in different magmas, we can infer the conditions under which they formed and the processes that have affected them.

For example, the enrichment of incompatible elements in shallow magmas suggests that these magmas may have undergone fractional crystallization, where the solid phases that formed at depth were removed from the liquid, leading to an enrichment of incompatible elements in the remaining liquid. On the other hand, the depletion of incompatible elements in high-temperature magmas suggests that these magmas may have undergone assimilation, where they incorporated crustal material that was depleted in these elements.

Furthermore, the pressure and temperature effects on trace element partitioning can also be used to interpret the evolution of magmatic systems. By examining the variations in trace element abundances in different magmas from the same system, we can track changes in pressure and temperature and infer the processes that have occurred during the evolution of the system.

In conclusion, the effects of pressure and temperature on trace element partitioning in magma-rich systems have significant implications for understanding geochemical processes and interpreting the evolution of magmatic systems. By considering these effects, we can gain valuable insights into the sources, conditions, and processes that control the distribution of trace elements in magmas.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the variations in trace element abundance in magma-rich systems. We have learned that the composition of magma can greatly influence the abundance of trace elements, and that these variations can provide valuable insights into the processes occurring within the Earth's crust and mantle. We have also discussed the importance of understanding these variations in order to accurately interpret geochemical data and make informed conclusions about the evolution of magmatic systems.

One key takeaway from this chapter is the concept of incompatible elements. These elements have a strong affinity for the melt phase and are often enriched in magmas, leading to significant variations in their abundance. This can be seen in the case of highly evolved magmas, where incompatible elements such as uranium and thorium can reach concentrations that are orders of magnitude higher than in the Earth's crust. This highlights the importance of considering the composition of the magma when interpreting trace element data.

Another important aspect discussed in this chapter is the role of fractional crystallization in trace element variations. As magma cools and crystallizes, certain elements may become preferentially incorporated into the solid phase, leading to changes in the abundance of these elements in the remaining melt. This process can also result in the formation of mineral deposits enriched in certain trace elements, which can have economic significance.

Overall, the study of trace element abundance variations in magma-rich systems is crucial for understanding the processes that shape our planet. By carefully analyzing and interpreting geochemical data, we can gain a better understanding of the Earth's composition and evolution. This knowledge is not only important for academic research, but also has practical applications in fields such as mineral exploration and resource management.

## Chapter: Trace-Element Geochemistry Textbook
### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the variations in trace element abundance in metamorphic systems. Metamorphic systems are geological environments where rocks undergo changes in mineralogy, texture, and chemical composition due to high temperatures and pressures. These changes can result in the redistribution of trace elements, which are elements present in very small amounts in rocks and minerals. Understanding the variations in trace element abundance in metamorphic systems is crucial for interpreting the geological history of a region and for identifying potential mineral resources.

We will begin by discussing the sources of trace elements in metamorphic systems, including their origins in the protolith (the original rock before metamorphism) and their introduction through fluids and melts. We will then explore the processes that control the distribution of trace elements during metamorphism, such as diffusion, fluid-rock interactions, and mineral reactions. This will provide a foundation for understanding the variations in trace element abundance that we will examine in the following sections.

Next, we will delve into the different types of metamorphic systems and how they can affect trace element abundance. This will include high-grade metamorphism, where rocks are subjected to high temperatures and pressures, and low-grade metamorphism, which occurs at lower temperatures and pressures. We will also discuss the role of metamorphic facies, which are groups of minerals that form under specific temperature and pressure conditions, in controlling trace element abundance.

Finally, we will explore case studies of trace element variations in metamorphic systems, including examples from different types of metamorphic environments and different types of trace elements. This will provide a comprehensive understanding of the factors that influence trace element abundance in metamorphic systems and how they can be used to interpret geological processes. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the complexities of trace element geochemistry in metamorphic systems and how it contributes to our understanding of Earth's history.


### Section: 17.1 Metamorphic Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Metamorphic systems are geological environments where rocks undergo changes in mineralogy, texture, and chemical composition due to high temperatures and pressures. These changes can result in the redistribution of trace elements, which are elements present in very small amounts in rocks and minerals. In this section, we will explore the concept of metamorphic equilibrium and how it relates to the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. We will also discuss the process of fractional crystallization and its role in controlling trace element abundance.

#### 17.1a Basic Concepts

Before delving into the specifics of trace element abundance variations in metamorphic systems, it is important to understand some basic concepts. Metamorphic equilibrium refers to the state in which a rock has reached a balance between its mineral assemblage and the surrounding environment. This means that the minerals present in the rock are stable and will not undergo any further changes, unless the environmental conditions change. In other words, the rock has reached a state of thermodynamic equilibrium.

The concept of metamorphic equilibrium is crucial in understanding the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. When a rock is in equilibrium, the distribution of trace elements is controlled by the mineral assemblage and the surrounding fluids. This means that the trace elements will be incorporated into the minerals that are stable under the given conditions, and any changes in the environmental conditions can result in the redistribution of these elements.

One process that can cause changes in the environmental conditions is fractional crystallization. This is the process by which minerals crystallize from a magma or solution in a specific order, based on their melting points. As the minerals crystallize, they remove certain elements from the remaining melt or solution, resulting in changes in the trace element composition. This process is important in metamorphic systems because it can lead to the formation of new minerals and the redistribution of trace elements.

In summary, understanding the concept of metamorphic equilibrium and the process of fractional crystallization is crucial in understanding the variations in trace element abundance in metamorphic systems. In the following sections, we will explore how these concepts apply to different types of metamorphic systems and how they can influence the distribution of trace elements.


### Section: 17.1 Metamorphic Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Metamorphic systems are geological environments where rocks undergo changes in mineralogy, texture, and chemical composition due to high temperatures and pressures. These changes can result in the redistribution of trace elements, which are elements present in very small amounts in rocks and minerals. In this section, we will explore the concept of metamorphic equilibrium and how it relates to the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. We will also discuss the process of fractional crystallization and its role in controlling trace element abundance.

#### 17.1a Basic Concepts

Before delving into the specifics of trace element abundance variations in metamorphic systems, it is important to understand some basic concepts. Metamorphic equilibrium refers to the state in which a rock has reached a balance between its mineral assemblage and the surrounding environment. This means that the minerals present in the rock are stable and will not undergo any further changes, unless the environmental conditions change. In other words, the rock has reached a state of thermodynamic equilibrium.

The concept of metamorphic equilibrium is crucial in understanding the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. When a rock is in equilibrium, the distribution of trace elements is controlled by the mineral assemblage and the surrounding fluids. This means that the trace elements will be incorporated into the minerals that are stable under the given conditions, and any changes in the environmental conditions can result in the redistribution of these elements.

One process that can cause changes in the environmental conditions is fractional crystallization. This is the process by which minerals crystallize from a magma or solution in a specific order, based on their melting points. As the minerals crystallize, they remove certain elements from the remaining melt or solution, resulting in a change in the trace element abundance. This process is important in metamorphic systems because it can lead to the formation of new minerals and the redistribution of trace elements.

#### 17.1b Role in Trace Element Distribution

Fractional crystallization plays a significant role in controlling the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. As mentioned earlier, this process can lead to the formation of new minerals and the removal of certain elements from the remaining melt or solution. This can result in changes in the trace element abundance in the rock, as well as the formation of mineral assemblages that are not in equilibrium with the surrounding environment.

One example of this is the formation of garnet in metamorphic rocks. Garnet is a common mineral in metamorphic systems and has a high affinity for certain trace elements, such as rare earth elements. During fractional crystallization, garnet can incorporate these trace elements into its crystal structure, leading to their removal from the remaining melt or solution. This can result in a depletion of these elements in the rock and a corresponding increase in their abundance in the garnet.

In addition to affecting the distribution of trace elements, fractional crystallization can also influence the formation of metamorphic rocks. As minerals crystallize and are removed from the melt or solution, the remaining melt or solution becomes more enriched in certain elements. This can lead to the formation of different mineral assemblages and ultimately, different types of metamorphic rocks.

In conclusion, fractional crystallization plays a crucial role in controlling the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. By understanding this process, we can better interpret the trace element abundance variations in metamorphic rocks and gain insight into the formation of these rocks. 


### Section: 17.1 Metamorphic Equilibrium and Fractional Crystallization:

Metamorphic systems are geological environments where rocks undergo changes in mineralogy, texture, and chemical composition due to high temperatures and pressures. These changes can result in the redistribution of trace elements, which are elements present in very small amounts in rocks and minerals. In this section, we will explore the concept of metamorphic equilibrium and how it relates to the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. We will also discuss the process of fractional crystallization and its role in controlling trace element abundance.

#### 17.1a Basic Concepts

Before delving into the specifics of trace element abundance variations in metamorphic systems, it is important to understand some basic concepts. Metamorphic equilibrium refers to the state in which a rock has reached a balance between its mineral assemblage and the surrounding environment. This means that the minerals present in the rock are stable and will not undergo any further changes, unless the environmental conditions change. In other words, the rock has reached a state of thermodynamic equilibrium.

The concept of metamorphic equilibrium is crucial in understanding the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. When a rock is in equilibrium, the distribution of trace elements is controlled by the mineral assemblage and the surrounding fluids. This means that the trace elements will be incorporated into the minerals that are stable under the given conditions, and any changes in the environmental conditions can result in the redistribution of these elements.

This is the process by which minerals crystallize from a magma or solution in a specific order, based on their melting points. As the minerals crystallize, they remove certain elements from the remaining melt or solution, resulting in a change in the trace element abundance. This process is important in metamorphic systems because it can lead to the formation of new minerals and the redistribution of trace elements.

#### 17.1b Metamorphic Equilibrium and Trace Element Abundance

As mentioned earlier, the concept of metamorphic equilibrium is crucial in understanding the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. When a rock is in equilibrium, the trace elements will be incorporated into the minerals that are stable under the given conditions. This means that the trace element abundance will vary depending on the mineral assemblage present in the rock.

For example, if a rock is in equilibrium with a fluid that is rich in a certain trace element, that element will be incorporated into the minerals that are stable under those conditions. On the other hand, if the fluid is depleted in that trace element, it will not be incorporated into the minerals. This can result in significant variations in trace element abundance within a single rock, depending on the mineral assemblage and the surrounding fluid.

#### 17.1c Case Studies

To further illustrate the concept of metamorphic equilibrium and its relationship to trace element abundance, let's look at some case studies. One example is the metamorphic rocks in the Franciscan Complex in California. These rocks have undergone high-grade metamorphism and contain a variety of minerals such as garnet, amphibole, and plagioclase. The trace element abundance in these rocks varies depending on the mineral assemblage, with some minerals containing high concentrations of certain elements while others have lower concentrations.

Another example is the metamorphic rocks in the Scottish Highlands. These rocks have undergone low-grade metamorphism and contain minerals such as chlorite, biotite, and muscovite. The trace element abundance in these rocks also varies depending on the mineral assemblage, with some minerals containing high concentrations of certain elements while others have lower concentrations.

These case studies demonstrate how the concept of metamorphic equilibrium can help us understand the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems. By studying the mineral assemblage and the surrounding fluids, we can gain insight into the variations in trace element abundance within a single rock. This information is crucial in understanding the processes that control the distribution of trace elements in metamorphic systems.
